V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">10.83</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">12.14</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="×">6.67&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–14</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">3D 50% V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">21.87</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">15.45</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="×">4.11&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–14</sup></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performances of various electrodes are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>. When measured at 2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, the 2D Cu foil exhibits obvious and irregular fluctuations in the plating/stripping curves, and it is short-circuited after 320 h. The rGO-CNT, 10% V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT, and 50% V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid electrodes can maintain the cycle for 700, 1140, and 726 h, respectively, and then are short-circuited. The 3D-printed 30% V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid electrode exhibits the stablest cycle performance with 1700 cycles. In addition, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, the 3D-printed 30% V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid electrode could maintain a microporous structure with uniformly deposited Na metal after 100 cycles at 2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, indicating the uniform Na ion flux by the sodiophilic interface constructed by V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene with strong mechanical strength provided by the rGO-CNT scaffold to support the whole electrode under the repeated plating/stripping process. Further measurement and investigation were carried out based on a 30% V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid electrode. More performance evaluation was performed at high current densities and large areal capacities to explore the application of the 3D-printed MXene structure. It is worth mentioning that it can cycle stably for more than 3000 and 1800 h at 2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with large capacities of 10 and 20 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14a</a>, respectively. At a high current density of 10 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, it can stably cycle for 140 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14b</a>). Furthermore, the capacity is increased to 50 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> at a current density of 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, and it can operate for 1000 h (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g). In order to show the advances of the 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrode, we compare the electrochemical performance of various MXene-based Na metal electrodes and 3D-printed Na metal electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h,i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S3 and S4</a>). It is can be seen that the 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrode exhibits the superior long cycle stability at high current densities and large areal capacities even up to 50 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Na metal plating/stripping behavior on the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid electrode was explored by investigating the surface morphology evolution <i>via</i><i>ex</i><i>situ</i> SEM characterization at 0.2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a–g). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a–c, during the Na metal deposition process, Na metal is gradually nucleated and grown uniformly on the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT nanosheet and gradually filled the microcavities, forming a flat surface without dendrite formation. For the rGO-CNT electrode, Na metal gradually filled the small and large pores between the rGO-CNT nanoflakes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15a–c</a>). However, for the 2D planar Cu electrode, plenty of mossy-like Na appears when the deposition capacity reached 0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, and obvious dendritic Na metal begins to appear and grow larger with the capacity reaching 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16a–c</a>). During the Na stripping process, Na metal slowly fades, and an obvious V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid structure emerges when the stripping capacity reaches 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f<sub>1</sub>). Na metal is also gradually stripped from the rGO-CNT electrode until a clearly porous structure appears when the stripped capacity reaches 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15d–f</a>). In contrast, on the Cu electrode, mossy-like Na metal begins to decrease. However, there are plenty of dendritic Na stacked on the Cu foil when stripped to 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16d–f</a>). From <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16g</a>, there is still 0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> Na metal not fully stripped, indicating plenty of dead Na metal is irreversible with a low CE of only 78.8%.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/medium/nn2c01186_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/large/nn2c01186_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Morphology evolution of Na deposition and stripping on 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrodes at 0.2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with the deposition capacities of (a, a<sub>1</sub>) 0.2, (b, b<sub>1</sub>) 0.5, and (c, c<sub>1</sub>) 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>; with the stripping capacities of (d, d<sub>1</sub>) 1, (e, e<sub>1</sub>) 1.5, and (f, f<sub>1</sub>) 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>; and (g) corresponding deposition–stripping curve. (h) <i>In situ</i> optical microscopy images of Na deposited on V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrodes at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 2 h.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/large/nn2c01186_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c01186&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A more visible morphology evolution of the Na metal deposition onto the Cu, rGO-CNT, and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrodes was performed by an <i>in situ</i> optical microscopy characterization technique at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h, respectively. After 60 min of deposition, large Na dendrites are grown on bare 2D planar Cu electrode, and they grow larger and larger until 120 min of deposition (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17a</a>). In contrast, there are some small particles grown on the top of the rGO-CNT electrode indicating that rGO-CNT can partially inhibit the growth of Na dendrites in a 60 min deposition (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17b</a>), which is consistent with a previous study. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> However, Na dendrites are still grown on the surface of the rGO-CNT electrode after 120 min of deposition. In contrast, the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT keeps its original porous structure without any Na dendrite formation even after 120 min of deposition (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h), indicating that the 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid aerogel can effectively inhibit the formation of Na dendrites.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further explore the revulsive effect of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene on Na metal deposition, <i>in situ</i> TEM characterization was carried out on V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrodes and the binding energy calculation based on DFT. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a shows a schematic diagram of an <i>in situ</i> TEM solid nanocell. The metallic Na at the tungsten end serves as the counter electrode, V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT on the copper wire serves as the working electrode, and Na<sub>2</sub>O formed by sodium exposed to air serves as a solid electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The tungsten tip rod is subjected to a bias of +3 V and gradually approaches the end of the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT nanoflakes, and the metallic Na particles are gradually nucleated with the center of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanoflakes and then grow larger and larger until merging into a large Na metal on top of the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT nanoflakes, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b. In detail, Na ions migrate through the GO platform to the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanoflakes (points 1–4 shown by the arrows at 21 s), and then tiny Na metals start to nucleate over these MXene nanoflakes. With the propagation of the deposition duration, more and more Na metals grow larger and larger with a uniform distribution over the surface of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> at 29 s of deposition. Some Na metal nucleates start to merge together after 64 s. A big Na metal appears at the bottom. This large Na metal merges with more and more Na nucleates until forming a giant Na metal at 170 s of deposition. Furthermore, compared with the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of the pristine MXene (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c), Na<sub>2</sub>O and NaOH diffraction patterns are generated after Na deposition (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d), which could be attributed to the inorganic compounds formed due to the sodium interacting with V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> surface functional groups (═O and −OH). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> A schematic diagram of the Na metal deposition process including nucleation, growth, and mergence/coalescence into a large Na metal is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e. The above results provide visible evidence of the Na metal deposition behavior onto the designed electrode with the guidance of sodiophilic V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene at the nanoscale level. The sodiophilicity of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene is further explored by calculating the binding energy and charge difference density simulations <i>via</i> DFT. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f–h, the binding energies of functional groups containing −F and −O to Na are −0.77 and −1.02 eV, respectively, which are much larger than that of carbon atoms on graphene (−0.06 eV). Additionally, charge difference density images further corroborate stronger interactions between Na ions and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> (T = −O, −F) than pure carbon. The DFT calculation results verify that the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene with abundant −O and −F groups demonstrates superior sodiophilicity, which would greatly facilitate Na deposition kinetics and reversibility. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref23" aria-label="reference 3 and 23">(3,23)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/medium/nn2c01186_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/large/nn2c01186_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Schematic diagram of the <i>in</i><i>situ</i> TEM setup. (b) <i>In</i><i>situ</i> TEM snapshots of the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT sodium plating process and SAED patterns of (c) pristine V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT and (d) after sodium deposition. (e) Schematic illustration of the Na deposition process. The binding energies between Na and (f) C (graphene), (g) F (V<sub>2</sub>CF), and (h) O (V<sub>2</sub>CO) calculated based on DFT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/large/nn2c01186_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c01186&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In light of these results and mechanism analysis, the excellent electrochemical performance of the 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrode can be ascribed to the following benefits. (1) 3D-printed rGO-CNT scaffolds provide high charge transfer speed routes <i>via</i> the interconnected conductive networks and the hierarchical porous structure for fast Na ion transportation, as well as a robust skeleton to support the whole electrode. (2) 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT not only provides a large surface area, which can partially inhibit Na metal dendrite formation owing to the reduced local current density distribution but also provide abundant nucleation centers and volume accommodation for Na metal deposition and growth. (3) Furthermore, V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene nanoflakes with plenty of sodiophilic functional groups can effectively guide the sodium metal nucleation and deposition owing to the strong affinity with Na metal, reducing the nucleation overpotential and inhibiting Na dendrite growth, allowing for long cycle stability even when measured at high current densities with large areal capacities. It is worth mentioning that the deposition–stripping curve at high current density with ultrahigh capacity (for example, 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 50 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g) starts to fluctuate after a long operation time, which may be related to the nonuniform deposition on the top of the 3D-printed nanostructure due to the large amount of Na deposition (50 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>) for a long operation time (∼1000 h). Although MXene can guide the Na metal uniform deposition, Na metal still prefers to deposit on the top surface of the 3D-printed electrode due to the top-growth effect especially at high current densities with large capacities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref52" aria-label="reference 1 and 52">(1,52)</a> The possible solutions to further improve the cycle stability include rational architecture design (MXene gradient distribution) to guide the Na metal deposited into the bottom of the electrode to inhibit the top-growth effect and more sodiophilic sites such as N doping introduced onto MXene to further improve the metal deposition. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 52 to 54">(52−54)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To further demonstrate the practical application of 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid aerogel electrodes, a full battery composed of Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT with 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> predeposited Na metal as the anode and 3D-printed NVP@C-rGO as the cathode was assembled (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a,b). The charge and discharge capacities of the Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT||NVP@C-rGO full cell are 95.69 and 94.02 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the sixth cycle at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). An LED is successfully powered by the full battery, as shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c. The rate capability of the full battery is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d. It can deliver discharge capacities of 91.02, 85.56, 77.50, 66.11, and 47.78 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. For the long cycle performance measurement, the full battery was cycled at a low current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for the first five cycles and then cycled at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The charge and discharge capacities of the full battery at the 90th cycle are 95.69 and 94.02 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In addition, this full battery can still deliver a high discharge capacity of 86.27 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a high CE of 99.41% after 400 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/medium/nn2c01186_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/large/nn2c01186_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT||NVP@C-rGO full battery electrochemical performance evaluation. (a) Schematic diagram of the full battery. (b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of the NVP@C-rGO cathode and Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT anode. (c) GCD curve of the full battery at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Inset is an LED powered by the full battery. (d) Rate performance of the full battery operated at the current density range of 100–2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Cycling performance and related CE of the full cell at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/asset/images/large/nn2c01186_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c01186&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid aerogels are successfully fabricated by a 3D printing technology and further adopted as the matrix for a Na metal anode. Hierarchical porous 3D-printed structures not only maintain the whole electrode structure stability during the extensive plating/stripping process but also provide the large surface area to reduce the current density and abundant active centers for Na metal nucleation, together with the enhanced Na-ion/electron transport kinetics. As evidenced by DFT simulations, sodiophilicity of the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene can be determined by the high binding energy between surface functional groups and Na ions. According to further <i>in situ</i> TEM analyses, sodiophilic V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene effectively guides the uniform Na nucleation and growth. Meanwhile, 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid aerogel electrodes can effectively reduce the Na nucleation overpotential and suppress the Na dendrite formation, hence contributing to a dendrite-free morphology during the multiple plating/stripping processes (proved by the <i>ex situ</i> SEM and <i>in</i><i>situ</i> optical microscopy characterization). As a result, the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrode can preserve a stable cycling over 3000 h with an average CE of 99.54% at 2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 10 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. Moreover, it can also stably operate for 1000 h even under harsh testing condition (5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 50 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>). Finally, a full battery assembled with an NVP@C-rGO cathode and Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT anode could deliver a high reversible capacity of 86.27 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Taken together, the present work offers a 3D printing strategy for the construction of a sodiophilic MXene matrix for improving Na metal plating/stripping chemistry.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> Nanosheets</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A 0.5 g amount of V<sub>2</sub>AlC (Jilin 11 Technology Co., Ltd.) powder was slowly added into 10 mL of HF acid under slow stirring at 50 °C for 72 h under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. The obtained residue was washed by deionized (DI) water and centrifuged several times until the pH value reached ∼6. The centrifuged precipitate was then combined with 4 mL of TBAOH (40 wt % in water, Sigma-Aldrich) and stirred for 24 h, followed by rinsing with DI water to remove the remaining TBAOH. TBA<sup>+</sup>-intercalated V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> residues were then combined with 50 mL of DI water and ultrasonicated for 1–2 h under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere with an ice bath, followed by centrifugation at 4000 rpm for 30 min. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The product was collected as the final V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets. Finally, a V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> aqueous solution was diluted to a concentration of 10 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Ink Formulation of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/GO-CNT for 3D Printing</h3><div class="NLM_p last">GO was prepared by a modified Hummer method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> A GO-CNT slurry was prepared by dispersing CNT (120 mg, purchased from Chinese Academy of Sciences Chengdu Organic Chemistry Co., LTD) into a GO aqueous solution (24 mL, 5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) with strong stirring for 48 h. V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/GO-CNT ink was prepared by adding the above V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> solution with various V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> mass ratios (10%, 30%, 50%) with stirring. The excess water was removed by high-speed centrifugation to prepare the printable V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/GO-CNT ink.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> 3D Printing of the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT Microgrid Aerogel</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The 3D printing process was performed by a multiaxis dispensing system (Musashi, SM200SX-3A) equipped with 3D programming software. The prepared slurry was loaded into a 5 mL syringe connected to a metal nozzle with a diameter of 150 μm. The air pressure was set at 0.15–0.2 MPa. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The pattern was printed onto a piece of glass slide with a speed of 5 mm s<sup>–1</sup>. The height between the nozzle and substrate was kept at 0.2 mm to ensure the adhesion of the printed filament and the adjacent printed layers. After the printing process, the printed microgrid electrode was frozen in a refrigerator overnight, then transferred into a freeze-dryer at −40 °C for 48 h to remove water. Subsequently, the freeze-dried sample was annealed in a tube furnace at 450 °C in an Ar gas environment for 2 h to reduce V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/GO-CNT to V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid aerogels.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Preparation of the NVP@C-rGO Cathode Material and Ink Formulation for 3D Printing</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The preparation process of NVP@C nanocomposites can be found in our previous studies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref56" aria-label="reference 37 and 56">(37,56)</a> The synthesized NVP@C nanocomposite (120 mg) was dispersed in a GO slurry (5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>, 24 mL) with a mass ratio of 1:1. The excess water was removed by high-speed centrifugation at 2000 rpm min<sup>–1</sup> for 20 min. The 3D printing process to fabricate the NVP@C-rGO microgrid aerogel is the same as that of the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT microgrid aerogels.</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the electrodes was characterized by SEM (JEOL, JSM-6700F, Japan) and TEM and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) (JEOL, JEM-2100, Japan). The EDS was performed by using the EDX module equipped on the SEM. The crystal structures of rGO-CNT and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT samples were analyzed by XRD (SmartLab 3 kW) at 40 kV using Cu Kα radiation. The Raman spectra of the samples were carried out by confocal Raman spectroscopy (Horiba, Labram HR Evolution). TGA was measured by a Discovery SDT650 (TA Instruments) from room temperature to 900 °C in air with a ramping rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. XPS qualitative analysis was carried out on a Thermo Fei 250Xi device. The surface area measurement was performed by N<sub>2</sub> physical adsorption at 77 K using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET, ASAP 2420, Micromeritics) method.</div></div><div id="sec4_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> <i>In Situ</i> Optical Microscopy Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The <i>in situ</i> optical microscopy measurement was carried out by a visualization cell with an optical microscope. A schematic diagram of the <i>in situ</i> optical cell is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>. The visualized cell was composed of the working electrode (Cu, rGO-CNT and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT), counter electrode (sodium foil), and a piece of physical separator. The cell was filled with 300 μL of electrolyte (1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme) and sealed with a piece of sapphire cover on the top in an Ar-filled glovebox with the oxygen and moisture level below 0.1 ppm. The morphology evolution of the working electrode in the visualization cell was recorded by an optical microscope (Cewei, LW750LJT).</div></div><div id="sec4_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> <i>In Situ</i> TEM Investigation</h3><div class="NLM_p last"><i>In situ</i> TEM tests were performed on a JEOL JEM-2100 TEM. The working electrode and counter electrode were V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT nanoflakes on a copper wire and metallic sodium on a tungsten tip, respectively. Na<sub>2</sub>O was naturally formed by sodium metal in air as a solid electrolyte. A piezoelectric actuator was used to drive Na/Na<sub>2</sub>O toward the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT nanoflakes, and a +3 V bias voltage was applied to the Na/Na<sub>2</sub>O electrode for plating the Na metal.</div></div><div id="sec4_8" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Electrochemical Deposition to Deposit the Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT Microgrid Anode and Performance Evaluation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT anode was prepared by a standard electrochemical deposition process. The V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT was used as the working electrode, and a piece of Na foil was used as the counter electrode. Celgard 2500 was used as the separator, and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme was used as the electrolyte. The above components were assembled into a CR2032 coin cell in an argon glovebox with a water oxygen content less than 0.1 ppm. Na was electrodeposited onto 3D-printed V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrodes by battery discharge/charging equipment (Neware, Shenzhen). Before electrodeposition, the cells were cycled five times at a current density of 50 μA cm<sup>–2</sup> in a voltage range of 0–1 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) to treat the surface and form a stable SEI film. The long cycle stability measurement was carried out at 2 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 1, 10, and 20 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, 10 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, and 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with 50 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. The EIS was measured by an electrochemical workstation (VMP3, Biologic) in the frequency range of 100k–0.01 Hz.</div></div><div id="sec4_9" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Computation Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The binding energy and charge difference density between Na and pure graphene and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> (T = −O, −F) were calculated by the DFT embedded in the Vienna Ab Initio Simulation Package (VASP) with the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 57 and 58">(57,58)</a> In this model, the exchange–correlation interaction between itinerant electrons was described by the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) function. The cutoff energy of all the calculations was 600 eV, and the <i>k</i>-point grid was set as 7 × 7 × 1. The energy and forces per atom were converged to within 10<sup>–5</sup> eV and 10<sup>–3</sup> eV Å<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. For the slab calculations, the vacuum thickness was chosen to be 15 Å to reduce artificial interactions due to periodic boundary conditions.</div></div><div id="sec4_10" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15"> Assembly of the Full Battery and Electrochemical Performance Evaluation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A full battery was assembled with NVP@C-rGO as the cathode and Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT as the anode. The Na@V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT anode was prepared by deposition of 2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> Na into the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrode. The mass loading of the cathode was approximately 8.32 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The full battery was assembled in a CR2032 coin cell in the glovebox, and the performance was evaluated in a potential window of 3.8–2.0 V. The capacity of the full cell was calculated based on the mass of the NVP@C.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional information on the SEM and TEM of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene, optical pictures, SEM, XPS, BET, electrochemical performance, EIS, and <i>in situ</i> optical microscope results of the 3D-printed electrodes, and performance comparison of our V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/rGO-CNT electrode with various Na metal anodes; Figures S1–S18; Tables S1–S4 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c01186/suppl_file/nn2c01186_si_001.pdf">nn2c01186_si_001.pdf (1.44 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.2c01186" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>119</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5416</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5460</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00642</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D119%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D5416-5460%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26author%3DB.%2BLee%26author%3DE.%2BPaek%26author%3DD.%2BMitlin%26author%3DS.%2BW.%2BLee%26title%3DSodium%2BMetal%2BAnodes%253A%2BEmerging%2BSolutions%2Bto%2BDendrite%2BGrowth%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemrev.8b00642&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.2c01186&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemrev.8b00642&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium metal anodes: Emerging solutions to dendrite growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Byeongyong; Paek, Eunsu; Mitlin, David; Lee, Seung Woo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">119</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5416-5460</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This comprehensive Review focuses on the key challenges and recent progress regarding sodium-metal anodes employed in sodium-metal batteries (SMBs).  The metal anode is the essential component of emerging energy storage systems such as sodium sulfur and sodium selenium, which are discussed as example full-cell applications.  We begin with a description of the differences in the chem. and phys. properties of Na metal vs. the oft-studied Li metal, and a corresponding discussion regarding the no. of ways in which Na does not follow Li-inherited paradigms in its electrochem. behavior.  We detail the major challenges for Na-metal systems that at this time limit the feasibility of SMBs.  The core Na anode problems are the following interrelated degrdn. mechanisms: An unstable solid electrolyte interphase with most org. electrolytes, "mossy" and "lath-like" metal dendrite growth for liq. systems, poor Coulombic efficiency, and gas evolution.  Even solid-state Na batteries are not immune, with metal dendrites being reported.  The solns. may be subdivided into the following interrelated taxonomy: Improved electrolytes and electrolyte additives tailored for Na-metal anodes, interfacial engineering between the metal and the liq. or solid electrolyte, electrode architectures that both reduce the c.d. during plating-stripping and serve as effective hosts that shield the Na metal from excessive reactions, and alloy design to tune the bulk properties of the metal per se.  For instance, stable plating-stripping of Na is extremely difficult with conventional carbonate solvents but has been reported with ethers and glymes.  Solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) such as beta-alumina solid electrolyte (BASE), sodium superionic conductor (NASICON), and sodium thiophosphate (75Na2S·25P2S5) present highly exciting opportunities for SMBs that avoid the dangers of flammable liqs.  Even SSEs are not immune to dendrites, however, which grow through the defects in the bulk pellet, but may be controlled through interfacial energy modification.  We conclude with a discussion of the key research areas that we feel are the most fruitful for further pursuit.  In our opinion, greatly improved understanding and control of the SEI structure is the key to cycling stability.  A holistic approach involving complementary post-mortem, in situ, and operando analyses to elucidate full battery cell level structure-performance relations is advocated.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqQ1buYt3eMx7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirFcuh0jWtDA"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Emerging rechargeable sodium-metal batteries (SMBs) are gaining extensive attention because of the high energy d., low cost, and promising potentials for large-scale applications.  The mechanism investigation and performance optimization of SMBs are of great significance for fundamental science and practical applications.  Consequently, this review provides fundamental insights into the cell chem. and recent progress on several representative SMBs, including Na-O2, Na-CO2, Na-SO2, and room-temp. Na-S batteries, for which the Na-storage mechanisms, potential solns. for enhancing battery performance, and future perspectives are discussed.  We emphasize the importance and challenges of sodium-metal anodes, as well as summarize and highlight feasible strategies to address the challenging issues facing them.  Combined with current research achievements, this review offers future research directions from the viewpoint of better SMB full cells regarding cathode design and anode protection with compatible electrolyte systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqB194LD5n6R7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirFcuh0jWtDA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Induction of Planar Sodium Growth on MXene (Ti3C2Tx)-Modified Carbon Cloth Hosts for Flexible Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Yongzheng; Lian, Ruqian; Li, Huipeng; Zhang, Ying; Gong, Zhe; Zhu, Kai; Ye, Ke; Yan, Jun; Wang, Guiling; Gao, Yu; Wei, Yingjin; Cao, Dianxue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium (Na) metal batteries have attracted increasing attention and gained rapid development.  However, the processing, storing, and application of Na metal anodes are restricted by its inherent stickiness and poor mech. properties.  Herein, an MXene (Ti3C2Tx)-coated carbon cloth (Ti3C2Tx-CC) host is designed and synthesized, which shows a highly metallic conductive and sodiophilic surface.  After a thermal infusion treatment, a Na-Ti3C2Tx-CC composite with rigidity and bendability is obtained and employed as a metal anode for Na ion batteries.  The Na-Ti3C2Tx-CC electrodes present stable cycling performance and high stripping/plating capacity in both an ether-based (up to 5 mA·h·cm-2) and a carbonate-based (up to 8 mA·h·cm-2) electrolyte.  The fundamental protection mechanism of MXene Ti3C2Tx is investigated.  Ti3C2Tx efficiently induces Na's initial nucleation and laterally oriented deposition, which effectively avoids the generation of mossy/dendritic Na.  The arrangement of Na atoms deposited on the MXene surface inherits the MXene at. architecture, leading to a smooth "sheet-like" Na surface.  Meanwhile, a flexible Na-based Na-Ti3C2Tx-CC‖Na3V2(PO4)3 device is assembled and exhibits capable electrochem. performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoo9WV202zRqrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liZujv6FOgxqA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tunable MXene-Derived 1D/2D Hybrid Nanoarchitectures as a Stable Matrix for Dendrite-Free and Ultrahigh Capacity Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Jianmin; Lu, Xuan; Matios, Edward; Wang, Chuanlong; Wang, Huan; Zhang, Yiwen; Hu, Xiaofei; Li, Weiyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although sodium (Na) is one of the most promising alternatives to lithium as an anode material for next-generation batteries, uncontrollable Na dendrite growth still remains the main challenge for Na metal batteries.  Herein, a novel 1D/2D Na3Ti5O12-MXene hybrid nanoarchitecture consisting of Na3Ti5O12 nanowires grown between the MXene nanosheets is synthesized by a facile approach using cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB)-pretreated Ti3C2 MXene.  Used as a matrix for the Na metal anode, the Na3Ti5O12 nanowires, formed benefiting from the CTAB stabilization, have chem. interaction with Na and thus provide abundant Na nucleation sites.  These 1D nanostructures, together with the unique confinement effect from the 2D nanosheets, effectively guide and control the Na deposition within the interconnected nanochannels, preventing the "hot spot" formation for dendrite growth.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-based batteries have attracted considerable attention and are recognized as ideal candidates for large-scale and low-cost energy storage.  Sodium (Na) metal anodes are considered as one of the most promising anodes for next-generation, high-energy, Na-based batteries owing to their high theor. specific capacity (1166 mA h g-1) and low std. electrode potential.  Herein, an overview of the recent developments in Na metal anodes for high-energy batteries is provided.  The high reactivity and large vol. expansion of Na metal anodes during charge and discharge make the electrode/electrolyte interphase unstable, leading to the formation of Na dendrites, short cycle life, and safety issues.  Design strategies to enable the efficient use of Na metal anodes are elucidated, including liq. electrolyte engineering, electrode/electrolyte interface optimization, sophisticated electrode construction, and solid electrolyte engineering.  Finally, the remaining challenges and future research directions are identified.  It is hoped that this progress report will shape a consistent view of this field and provide inspiration for future research to improve Na metal anodes and enable the development of high-energy sodium batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzjTrnnlAmYrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liZujv6FOgxqA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvFGhurfI&amp;md5=29c0356a4c9e4fb466d90b6c3c8c528b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2018.09.017"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent advances in effective protection of sodium metal anode</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>53</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">630</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">642</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent advances in effective protection of sodium metal anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Linlin; Li, Xifei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">53</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of the ultrahigh theor. capacity, sodium metal is regarded as the most prospective next-generation anode material.  However, due to the dendritic sodium formation as well as high chem. reactivity with electrolyte, it still has safety issues and poor cyclability, which have severely impeded the practicability of sodium metal technol.  Therefore, it is important for the advanced sodium metal batteries to construct the stable dendrite-free sodium metal anode.  In recent years, considerable efforts have been directed towards solving these problems, including sodium-electrode framework design, org. electrolyte modification, sodium-metal interface protection.  Furthermore, solid-state electrolytes have an advantage over the liq. electrolytes and are considered to constitute safe solid sodium metal batteries so as to prohibit problematic sodium dendrites.  This review comprehensively analyzes the growth mechanism of the sodium dendrites and summarizes recent advances in the strategies of suppressing the formation of sodium dendrite.  The practical application in full batteries and future challenges of sodium metal anode are addressed for energy storage devices.  We hope this review delivers an overall picture of sodium protection strategies and inspires more researches in the future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrEMYz-WMQl3bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixRbo3C9x9rQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhslKqur%252FE&amp;md5=a6bc161d3a17e1e3d6e4646647dcea8f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201802833"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">2D Materials for Lithium/Sodium Metal Anodes</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Zhang, Ying; Xu, Shaomao; Dai, Jiaqi; Hitz, Emily; Li, Yiju; Yang, Chunpeng; Chen, Chaoji; Liu, Boyang; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3792-3797</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted great attention because of the widely available, abundant sodium resources and potentially low cost.  Currently, the challenge of the NIB development is due primarily to the lack of a high-performance anode, while the Na metal anode holds great promise considering its highest specific capacity of 1165 mA h/g and lowest anodic potential.  However, an uneven deposit, relatively infinite vol. change, and dendritic growth upon plating/stripping cycles cause a low Coulombic efficiency, poor cycling performance, and severe safety concerns.  Here, a stable Na carbonized wood (Na-wood) composite anode was fabricated via a rapid melt infusion (about 5 s) into channels of carbonized wood by capillary action.  The channels function as a high-surface-area, conductive, mech. stable skeleton, which lowers the effective c.d., ensures a uniform Na nucleation, and restricts the vol. change over cycles.  As a result, the Na-wood composite anode exhibited flat plating/stripping profiles with smaller overpotentials and stable cycling performance over 500 h at 1.0 mA/cm2 in a common carbonate electrolyte system.  In sharp comparison, the planar Na metal electrode showed a much shorter cycle life of 100 h under the same test conditions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpADAW0UsArmLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixRbo3C9x9rQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXntVWqsro%253D&amp;md5=0b473da510eee4ea92693872db5c43db</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b03185"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X. Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Porous Al Current Collector for Dendrite-Free Na Metal Anodes</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>17</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5862</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5868</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b03185</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D17%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D5862-5868%26journal%3DNano%2BLett.%26author%3DS.%2BLiu%26author%3DS.%2BTang%26author%3DX.%2BY.%2BZhang%26author%3DA.%2BX.%2BWang%26author%3DQ.%2BH.%2BYang%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BLuo%26title%3DPorous%2BAl%2BCurrent%2BCollector%2Bfor%2BDendrite-Free%2BNa%2BMetal%2BAnodes%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.nanolett.7b03185&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.2c01186&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.7b03185&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Porous Al Current Collector for Dendrite-Free Na Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Shan; Tang, Shan; Zhang, Xinyue; Wang, Aoxuan; Yang, Quan-Hong; Luo, Jiayan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5862-5868</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-based batteries are proposed as promising energy storage candidates for beyond Li-ion technol. due to the higher natural earth of Na metal.  For its high capacity and low potential, Na metal may carve itself a niche when directly used as anodes.  Similar to or even more problematic than Li, however, uneven plating/stripping of Na leads to dendrite formation.  As the plating substrates, current collectors have a paramount influence on the Na plating/stripping behaviors.  Here we propose porous Al current collectors as the plating substrate to suppress Na dendrites.  Al does not alloy with Na.  It is advantageous over Cu current collectors in terms of cost and wt.  The interconnected porous structure can increase available surface for Na to nucleate and decrease the Na+ flux distribution, leading to homogeneous plating.  The Na metal anodes can run for over 1000 cycles on porous Al with a low and stable voltage hysteresis and their av. plating/stripping Coulombic efficiency was above 99.9%, which is greatly improved compared to planar Al.  We used the porous Al for Na-O2, Na-Na3V2(PO4)3 cells with low Na amt. and anode free Na-TiS2 batteries and anticipate that using this strategy can be combined with further electrolyte and cathodes to develop high performance Na-based batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrVngjnd3Lo8LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liLMAbFZAhKjQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtlWhtr7E&amp;md5=9a19c40e0f5f971ed38323d851d22c09</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201700783"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Z.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Free-Standing Hollow Carbon Fibers as High-Capacity Containers for Stable Lithium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Lin; Yin, Ya-Xia; Li, Jin-Yi; Li, Nian-Wu; Zeng, Xian-Xiang; Ye, Huan; Guo, Yu-Guo; Wan, Li-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Joule</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">563-575</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JOULBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2542-4351</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal has been deemed as the most attractive anode due to its high theor. capacity and low anode potential.  Unfortunately, its development still faces various challenges, including dendritic Li growth and low Coulombic efficiency.  Here, we demonstrate that a light-wt., flexible, and free-standing 3D hollow carbon fiber (3D-HCF) container with high electroactive surface area can significantly reduce local c.d. and improve the Li deposition behavior.  Li is confined within the interspace among the fibers and inside the hollow tubular fibers without uncontrollable Li dendrites.  The Li anode in the 3D-HCFs exhibits high Coulombic efficiency (∼99.5% over 350 cycles), large areal capacity, and long-running lifespan (&gt;1,200 h) with an exceptionally low overpotential (&lt;20 mV).  A full cell with a LiFePO4 cathode shows flat voltage profiles and good cycle life.  We expect this work to inspire other Li container designs to promote the development of Li metal anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGql3mNJun_ya7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liLMAbFZAhKjQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXpsl2ku7s%253D&amp;md5=a9b91bec4af7cbf4e6f05d428df66930</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201802282"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matios, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Chemically Engineered Porous Copper Matrix with Cylindrical Core-Shell Skeleton as a Stable Host for Metallic Sodium Anodes</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Dendrite-Free Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by a Sodium Benzenedithiolate-Rich Protection Layer</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Ming; Wang, Guanyao; Liu, Xing; Guo, Bingkun; Xu, Gang; Huang, Zhongyi; Wu, Minghong; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue; Wu, Chao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),

                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium metal is an ideal anode material for metal rechargeable batteries, owing to its high theor. capacity (1166 mAh g-1), low cost, and earth-abundance.  However, the dendritic growth upon Na plating, stemming from unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film, is a major and most notable problem.  Here, a sodium benzenedithiolate (PhS2Na2)-rich protection layer is synthesized in situ on sodium by a facile method that effectively prevents dendrite growth in the carbonate electrolyte, leading to stabilized sodium metal electrodeposition for 400 cycles (800 h) of repeated plating/stripping at a c.d. of 1 mA cm-2.  The org. salt, PhS2Na2, is found to be a crit. component in the protection layer.  This finding opens up a new and promising avenue, based on org. sodium salts, to stabilize sodium metals with a protection layer.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHfbxFB8D1GbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liLMAbFZAhKjQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXjt12ns78%253D&amp;md5=6f5ceac46f3abef04c4355eee46bfe9f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1126/science.aag2421"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shahzad, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alhabeb, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hatter, C.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electromagnetic interference shielding with 2D transition metal carbides (MXenes)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shahzad, Faisal; Alhabeb, Mohamed; Hatter, Christine B.; Anasori, Babak; Hong, Soon Man; Koo, Chong Min; Gogotsi, Yury</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">353</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6304</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1137-1140</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0036-8075</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Materials with good flexibility and high cond. that can provide electromagnetic interference (EMI) shielding with minimal thickness are highly desirable, esp. if they can be easily processed into films.  Two-dimensional metal carbides and nitrides, known as MXenes, combine metallic cond. and hydrophilic surfaces.  Here, we demonstrate the potential of several MXenes and their polymer composites for EMI shielding.  A 45-μm-thick Ti3C2Tx film exhibited EMI shielding effectiveness of 92 decibels (&gt;50 decibels for a 2.5-μm film), which is the highest among synthetic materials of comparable thickness produced to date.  This performance originates from the excellent elec. cond. of Ti3C2Tx films (4600 S per cm) and multiple internal reflections from Ti3C2Tx flakes in free-standing films.  The mech. flexibility and easy coating capability offered by MXenes and their composites enable them to shield surfaces of any shape while providing high EMI shielding efficiency.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGosyOTrqtk3TrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgusPuH43jyrQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsVKjsL%252FJ&amp;md5=36061e4a710fd4b24cbb4fb8dcd6f80f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201704561"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, X.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Self-Healable 3D Microsupercapacitor with MXene-Graphene Composite Aerogel</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yue, Yang; Liu, Nishuang; Ma, Yanan; Wang, Siliang; Liu, Weijie; Luo, Cheng; Zhang, Hang; Cheng, Feng; Rao, Jiangyu; Hu, Xiaokang; Su, Jun; Gao, Yihua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4224-4232</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-performance microsupercapacitors (MSCs) with three-dimensional (3D) structure provide an effective approach to improve the ability of energy storage.  Because the electrodes with 3D structure are generally easily destroyed under mech. deformation in practical applications, we fabricated a self-healable 3D MSC consisting of MXene (Ti3C2Tx)-graphene (reduced graphene oxide, rGO) composite aerogel electrode by wrapping it with a self-healing polyurethane as an outer shell.  The MXene-rGO composite aerogel combining large sp. surface area of rGO and high cond. of the MXene can not only prevent the self-restacking of the lamella structure but also resist the poor oxidization of MXene to a degree.  The MSC based on a 3D MXene-rGO aerogel delivers a large area specific capacitance of 34.6 mF cm-2 at a scan rate of 1 mV s-1 and an outstanding cycling performance with a capacitance retention up to 91% over 15,000 cycles.  The 3D MSC presents an excellent self-healing ability with specific capacitance retention of 81.7% after the fifth healing.  The prepn. of this self-healable 3D MSC can provide a method for designing and manufg. next-generation long-life multifunctional electronic devices further to meet the requirements of sustainable development.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoaeIa2GHEyLrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgusPuH43jyrQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXnsF2kuro%253D&amp;md5=d7c70164c8e2bf6fba88da6a51d0b90d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.9b03650"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Z.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Wholly Degradable, Rechargeable Zn-Ti3C2 MXene Capacitor with Superior Anti-Self-Discharge Function</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Qi; Huang, Zhaodong; Li, Xinliang; Liu, Zhuoxin; Li, Hongfei; Liang, Guojin; Wang, Donghong; Huang, Qing; Zhang, Suojiang; Chen, Shimou; Zhi, Chunyi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8275-8283</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Degradable energy storage systems (ESSs) have been proposed to tackle increasing e-wastes such as heavy metals and toxic org. electrolytes.  However, currently reported degradable ESSs are scarce because it is very difficult to make all of the electrochem. components degradable as they must be stable for energy storage.  Here, an all-component degradable and rechargeable Zn-MXene capacitor is designed with outstanding anti-self-discharge function using zinc nanosheets and Ti3C2 MXene as electrodes.  The whole capacitor can retain ca. 82.5% of the capacitance after 1000 cycles and be totally degraded within 7.25 days, comprehensively surpassing the current degradable supercapacitors (120 days, 400 cycles) and batteries (19 days, 0-20 cycles).  In addn., while supercapacitors are notorious for intensive self-discharge, the Zn-MXene capacitor demonstrated the lowest self-discharge rate of 6.4 mV h-1, better than all the previous supercapacitors with specifically designed anti-self-discharge components including electrodes (&gt; 300 mV h-1), electrolytes (12-50 mV h-1), and separators (20-400 mV h-1).  This is illustrated by the as-proposed "static electricity-immune mechanism" which refers to breaking the electrostatic adsorption.  This Zn-MXene capacitor represents a great advance in degradable rechargeable ESSs and provides a strategy to fundamentally overcome the self-discharge problem encountered by supercapacitors.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHcH_aHbYV7LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCfKv44ILAHg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXht1WisbjF&amp;md5=bce13defc8d4c6fcbf21d852ac9b721b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.9b06394"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gogotsi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Anasori, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Rise of MXenes</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>13</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">8491</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">8494</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Prediction and Characterization of MXene Nanosheet Anodes for Non-Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xie, Yu; Dall'Agnese, Yohan; Naguib, Michael; Gogotsi, Yury; Barsoum, Michel W.; Zhuang, Houlong L.; Kent, Paul R. C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9606-9615</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable non-lithium-ion (Na+, K+, Mg2+, Ca2+, and Al3+) batteries have attracted attention as emerging low-cost and high energy-d. technologies for large-scale renewable energy storage.  However, the development of these batteries is hindered by the limited choice of high-performance electrode materials.  MXene nanosheets, a class of 2-dimensional transition metal carbides, are predicted to serve as high-performing anodes for non-lithium-ion batteries by combined 1st-principles simulations and exptl. measurements.  Both O-terminated and bare MXenes are promising anode materials with high capacities and good rate capabilities, while bare MXenes show better performance.  The expts. clearly demonstrate the feasibility of Na- and K-ion intercalation into terminated MXenes.  Also, stable multilayer adsorption is predicted for Mg and Al, which significantly increases their theor. capacities.  Also O-terminated MXenes can decomp. into bare MXenes and metal oxides when in contact with Mg, Ca, or Al.  The results provide insight into metal ion storage mechanisms on 2-dimensional materials and suggest a route to prepg. bare MXene nanosheets.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq7z7aEpCso9bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhCfKv44ILAHg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithiophilic Three-Dimensional Porous Ti3C2Tx-rGO Membrane as a Stable Scaffold for Safe Alkali Metal (Li or Na) Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Yongzheng; Zhang, Ying; Zhu, Kai; Lian, Ruqian; Gao, Yu; Yin, Jinling; Ye, Ke; Cheng, Kui; Yan, Jun; Wang, Guiling; Wei, Yingjin; Cao, Dianxue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
